<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">God</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the concept of a supreme "God" in the context of <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a>. For the general concept of a being superior to humans that is worshiped as "a god", see <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">Deity</a>. For God in specific religions, see <a href="#In_specific_religions">§ In specific religions</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/God_(disambiguation)" title="God (disambiguation)">God (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:117px;max-width:117px">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:137px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vishnu.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="246" data-file-width="205" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Vishnu.jpg/115px-Vishnu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Vishnu.jpg/173px-Vishnu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a6/Vishnu.jpg 2x" width="115"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Although God is usually thought of as an intangible spirit, and thus has no physical or even visual form, many religions use images to "represent" God in icons for art or for worship. Here are examples of representations of God in different monotheistic religions. Clockwise from upper left: <a href="/wiki/God_in_Christianity" title="God in Christianity">Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaumaram" title="Kaumaram">Kaumaram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shaktism" title="Shaktism">Shaktism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavism</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monad.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="256" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Monad.svg/220px-Monad.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Monad.svg/330px-Monad.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Monad.svg/440px-Monad.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monad.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Circled_dot" title="Circled dot">circled dot</a>, an ancient symbol for the metaphysical <a href="/wiki/Absolute_(philosophy)" title="Absolute (philosophy)">Absolute</a>. Early <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Astrology_and_astronomy" title="Astrology and astronomy">astrology and astronomy</a>, was connected to the divine for most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_science_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="History of science in the Middle Ages">medieval scholars</a>, and many believed that there was something intrinsically "divine" or "perfect" that could be found in circles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic</a> thought, <b>God</b> is conceived of as the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Being" title="Supreme Being">Supreme Being</a> and the principal object of <a href="/wiki/Faith#religious_views" title="Faith">faith</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-3">[3]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Conceptions_of_God" title="Conceptions of God">concept of God</a>, as described by <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theologians</a>, commonly includes the attributes of <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">omniscience</a> (all-knowing), <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">omnipotence</a> (unlimited power), <a href="/wiki/Omnipresence" title="Omnipresence">omnipresence</a> (present everywhere), <a href="/wiki/Divine_simplicity" title="Divine simplicity">divine simplicity</a>, and as having an eternal and necessary existence. Many theologians also describe God as being <a href="/wiki/Omnibenevolence" title="Omnibenevolence">omnibenevolent</a> (perfectly good) and <a href="/wiki/Love_of_God" title="Love of God">all loving</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Reliable source needed for the whole sentence (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>God is most often held to be <a href="/wiki/Incorporeality" title="Incorporeality">incorporeal</a> (immaterial),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-3">[3]</a></sup> and to be without gender,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> although many religions describe God using masculine terminology, using such terms as "Him" or "Father" and some religions (such as <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>) attribute only a purely grammatical <a href="/wiki/Gender_of_God" title="Gender of God">"gender" to God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Incorporeity and corporeity of God are related to conceptions of <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendence</a> (being outside nature) and <a href="/wiki/Immanence" title="Immanence">immanence</a> (being in nature, in the world) of God, with positions of synthesis such as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immanent_transcendence" title="Immanent transcendence">immanent transcendence</a>" of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_theology" title="Chinese theology">Chinese theology</a>.</p>
<p>God has been conceived as either <a href="/wiki/Personal_god" title="Personal god">personal</a> or impersonal. In <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theism</a>, God is the <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">creator</a> and <a href="/wiki/God_the_Sustainer" title="God the Sustainer">sustainer</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>, while in <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a>, God is the creator, but not the sustainer, of the universe. In <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheism</a>, God is the universe itself. In <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a>, God is not believed to exist, while God is deemed unknown or unknowable within the context of <a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">agnosticism</a>. God has also been conceived as the source of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moral_obligation" title="Moral obligation">moral obligation</a>, and the "greatest conceivable existent".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-3">[3]</a></sup> Many notable philosophers have developed arguments for and against the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platinga_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Platinga-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The many different conceptions of God, and competing claims as to God's characteristics, aims, and actions, have led to the development of ideas of <a href="/wiki/Omnitheism" title="Omnitheism">omnitheism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pandeism" title="Pandeism">pandeism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lataster_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lataster-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawe_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawe-9">[9]</a></sup> or a <a href="/wiki/Perennial_philosophy" title="Perennial philosophy">perennial philosophy</a>, which postulates that there is one underlying theological truth, of which all religions express a partial understanding, and as to which "the devout in the various great world religions are in fact worshipping that one God, but through different, overlapping concepts or mental images of Him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are many <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God" title="Names of God">names for God</a>, and different names are attached to different cultural ideas about God's identity and attributes. In the ancient Egyptian era of <a href="/wiki/Atenism" title="Atenism">Atenism</a>, possibly the earliest recorded monotheistic religion, this deity was called <a href="/wiki/Aten" title="Aten">Aten</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> premised on being the one "true" Supreme Being and creator of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, "He Who Is", "<a href="/wiki/I_Am_that_I_Am" title="I Am that I Am">I Am that I Am</a>", and the <a href="/wiki/Tetragrammaton" title="Tetragrammaton">tetragrammaton</a> YHWH (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="he">יהוה</span>‎‎, which means: "I am who I am"; "He Who Exists") are used as names of God, while <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">Yahweh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jehovah" title="Jehovah">Jehovah</a> are sometimes used in Christianity as vocalizations of YHWH. In the Christian doctrine of the Trinity, God, consubstantial in three persons, is called the <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">Father</a>, the <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">Son</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">Holy Spirit</a>. In Judaism, it is common to refer to God by the titular <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Judaism" title="Names of God in Judaism">names</a> <a href="/wiki/Elohim" title="Elohim">Elohim</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adonai" title="Adonai">Adonai</a>, the latter of which is believed by some scholars to descend from the Egyptian Aten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-freud_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-freud-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, the name <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a> is used, while Muslims also have a <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Islam" title="Names of God in Islam">multitude of titular names</a> for God. In <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> is often considered a <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monistic</a> concept of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_religion" title="Chinese religion">Chinese religion</a>, God is conceived as the <a href="/wiki/Progenitor" title="Progenitor">progenitor</a> (first ancestor) of the universe, intrinsic to it and constantly <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">ordaining</a> it. Other religions have names for God, for instance, <a href="/wiki/God_in_the_Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="God in the Bahá'í Faith">Baha</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá'í Faith">Bahá'í Faith</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Waheguru" title="Waheguru">Waheguru</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ahura_Mazda" title="Ahura Mazda">Ahura Mazda</a> in <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_usage">Etymology and usage</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louvre_042010_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1077" data-file-width="808" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Louvre_042010_01.jpg/220px-Louvre_042010_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Louvre_042010_01.jpg/330px-Louvre_042010_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Louvre_042010_01.jpg/440px-Louvre_042010_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louvre_042010_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Mesha_Stele" title="Mesha Stele">Mesha Stele</a> bears the earliest known reference (840 BCE) to the Israelite God Yahweh.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/God_(word)" title="God (word)">God (word)</a></div>
<p>The earliest written form of the Germanic word <i>God</i> (always, in this usage, <a href="/wiki/Capitalization_in_English" title="Capitalization in English">capitalized</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>) comes from the 6th-century <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Codex_Argenteus" title="Codex Argenteus">Codex Argenteus</a></i>. The English word itself is derived from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> * ǥuđan. The reconstructed <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> form <span class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space: nowrap" title="Proto-Indo-European language">* ǵhu-tó-m</span> was likely based on the root <span class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space: nowrap" title="Proto-Indo-European language">* ǵhau(ə)-</span>, which meant either "to call" or "to invoke".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The Germanic words for <i>God</i> were originally <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">neuter</a>—applying to both genders—but during the process of the <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">Christianization</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_people" title="Germanic people">Germanic peoples</a> from their indigenous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">Germanic paganism</a>, the words became a <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">masculine syntactic form</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BARNHART323_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-BARNHART323-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allah3.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="213" data-file-width="213" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Allah3.svg/220px-Allah3.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Allah3.svg/330px-Allah3.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Allah3.svg/440px-Allah3.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allah3.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The word 'Allah' in <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">Arabic calligraphy</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a>, capitalization is used for names by which a god is known, including 'God'. Consequently, the capitalized form of <i>god</i> is not used for multiple gods (<a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheism</a>) or when used to refer to the generic idea of a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The English word <i>God</i> and its counterparts in other languages are normally used for any and all conceptions and, in spite of significant differences between religions, the term remains an English translation common to all. The same holds for Hebrew <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_(god)" title="El (god)">El</a></i>, but <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Judaism" title="Names of God in Judaism">in Judaism</a>, God is also given a proper name, the <a href="/wiki/Tetragrammaton" title="Tetragrammaton">tetragrammaton</a> YHWH, in origin possibly the name of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edomite" title="Edomite">Edomite</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midianite" title="Midianite">Midianite</a> deity, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yahweh_(Canaanite_deity)" title="Yahweh (Canaanite deity)">Yahweh</a>. In many translations of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, when the word <i>LORD</i> is in all capitals, it signifies that the word represents the tetragrammaton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barton2006_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barton2006-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All%C4%81h" title="Allāh">Allāh</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">الله</span>‎‎) is the <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> term with no <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a> used by Muslims and Arabic speaking Christians and Jews meaning "The God" (with a capital G), while "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CA%BEil%C4%81h" title="ʾilāh">ʾilāh</a>" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><b>إله</b></span>‎‎) is the term used for a deity or a god in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> God may also be given a proper name in monotheistic currents of Hinduism which emphasize the <a href="/wiki/Personal_god" title="Personal god">personal nature of God</a>, with early references to his name as <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a>-<a href="/wiki/Vasudeva" title="Vasudeva">Vasudeva</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bhagavata" title="Bhagavata">Bhagavata</a> or later <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hari" title="Hari">Hari</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hastings541_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hastings541-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ahura Mazda is the name for God used in Zoroastrianism. "Mazda", or rather the Avestan stem-form <i>Mazdā-</i>, nominative <i>Mazdå</i>, reflects Proto-Iranian <i>*Mazdāh (female)</i>. It is generally taken to be the proper name of the spirit, and like its <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> cognate <i>medhā</i>, means "<a href="/wiki/Intelligence" title="Intelligence">intelligence</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Wisdom" title="Wisdom">wisdom</a>". Both the Avestan and Sanskrit words reflect <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Iranian_language" title="Proto-Indo-Iranian language">Proto-Indo-Iranian</a> <i>*mazdhā-</i>, from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> mn̩sdʰeh<sub>1</sub><i>, literally meaning "placing (</i>dʰeh<sub>1</sub><i>) one's mind (</i>*mn̩-s<i>)", hence "wise".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyce1983685_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyce1983685-32">[32]</a></sup></i></p>
<p>Waheguru (<a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>: <span lang="pa"><i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit Transliteration">vāhigurū</span></i></span>) is a term most often used in <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a> to refer to God. It means "Wonderful Teacher" in the Punjabi language. <i>Vāhi</i> (a Middle Persian borrowing) means "wonderful" and <i><a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">guru</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa"><i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit Transliteration">guru</span></i></span>) is a term denoting "teacher". Waheguru is also described by some as an experience of ecstasy which is beyond all descriptions. The most common usage of the word "Waheguru" is in the greeting Sikhs use with each other:</p>
<blockquote>
<div class="poem">
<p><i>Waheguru Ji Ka Khalsa, Waheguru Ji Ki Fateh</i><br/>
Wonderful Lord's <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a>, Victory is to the Wonderful Lord.</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p><i>Baha</i>, the "greatest" name for God in the Baha'i faith, is Arabic for "All-Glorious".</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General_conceptions">General conceptions</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conceptions_of_God" title="Conceptions of God">Conceptions of God</a></div>
<p>There is no clear consensus on the nature or even the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abrahamic_conceptions_of_God" title="Abrahamic conceptions of God">Abrahamic conceptions of God</a> include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotheistic" title="Monotheistic">monotheistic</a> definition of God in <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">trinitarian</a> view of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_concept_of_God" title="Islamic concept of God">Islamic concept of God</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dharmic_religions" title="Dharmic religions">dharmic religions</a> differ in their view of the divine: views of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Hinduism" title="God in Hinduism">God in Hinduism</a> vary by region, sect, and caste, ranging from monotheistic to polytheistic. Many polytheistic religions share the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">creator deity</a>, though having a name other than "God" and without all of the other roles attributed to a singular God by monotheistic religions. <a href="/wiki/Jainism_and_non-creationism" title="Jainism and non-creationism">Jainism</a> is [polytheistic]] and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-creationist" title="Non-creationist">non-creationist</a>. Depending on one's interpretation and tradition, Buddhism can be conceived as being either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheistic" title="Atheistic">atheistic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-theistic" title="Non-theistic">non-theistic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheistic" title="Pantheistic">pantheistic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panentheistic" title="Panentheistic">panentheistic</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytheistic" title="Polytheistic">polytheistic</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oneness">Oneness</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">Monotheism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henotheism" title="Henotheism">Henotheism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="468" data-file-width="520" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/220px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/330px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/440px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Trinity is the belief that God is composed of <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">The Father</a>, <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">The Son</a> (embodied metaphysically in the physical realm by <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">The Holy Spirit</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">Monotheists</a> hold that there is only one god, and may claim that the one true god is worshiped in different religions under different names. The view that all theists actually worship the same god, whether they know it or not, is especially emphasized in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_the_Trinity" title="Doctrine of the Trinity">doctrine of the Trinity</a> describes God as one God in three persons. The Trinity comprises <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">The Father</a>, <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">The Son</a> (embodied metaphysically by <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">The Holy Spirit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>'s most fundamental concept is <i><a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">tawhid</a></i> (meaning "oneness" or "uniqueness"). God is described in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> as: "Say: He is Allah, the One and Only; Allah, the Eternal, Absolute; He begetteth not, nor is He begotten; And there is none like unto Him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Muslims repudiate the Christian doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> and the divinity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_Jesus" title="Islamic view of Jesus">Jesus</a>, comparing it to <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheism</a>. In Islam, God is beyond all comprehension or equal and does not resemble any of his creations in any way. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> are not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iconodules" title="Iconodules">iconodules</a>, and are not expected to visualize God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Henotheism" title="Henotheism">Henotheism</a> is the belief and worship of a single god while accepting the existence or possible existence of other <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Theism,_deism_and_pantheism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Theism.2C_deism_and_pantheism">Theism, deism and pantheism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">Theism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">Deism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">Pantheism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">Theism</a> generally holds that God exists realistically, objectively, and independently of human thought; that God created and sustains everything; that God is omnipotent and eternal; and that God is personal and interacting with the universe through, for example, <a href="/wiki/Religious_experience" title="Religious experience">religious experience</a> and the prayers of humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smart_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-smart-41">[41]</a></sup> Theism holds that God is both transcendent and immanent; thus, God is simultaneously infinite and, in some way, present in the affairs of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lemos_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-lemos-42">[42]</a></sup> Not all theists subscribe to all of these propositions, but each usually subscribes to some of them (see, by way of comparison, <a href="/wiki/Family_resemblance" title="Family resemblance">family resemblance</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smart_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-smart-41">[41]</a></sup> Catholic theology holds that God is <a href="/wiki/Divine_simplicity" title="Divine simplicity">infinitely simple</a> and is not involuntarily subject to time. Most theists hold that God is omnipotent, omniscient, and benevolent, although this belief raises questions about God's responsibility for evil and suffering in the world. Some theists ascribe to God a self-conscious or purposeful limiting of omnipotence, omniscience, or benevolence. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_Theism" title="Open Theism">Open Theism</a>, by contrast, contends that, due to the nature of time, God's omniscience does not mean the deity can predict the future. <i>Theism</i> is sometimes used to refer in general to any belief in a god or gods, i.e., monotheism or <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philosofrelGlossthe_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-philosofrelGlossthe-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TFDtheism_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-TFDtheism-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blake_God_Blessing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2401" data-file-width="2000" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Blake_God_Blessing.jpg/200px-Blake_God_Blessing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Blake_God_Blessing.jpg/300px-Blake_God_Blessing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Blake_God_Blessing.jpg/400px-Blake_God_Blessing.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blake_God_Blessing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"God blessing the <a href="/wiki/Sabbath" title="Sabbath">seventh day</a>", a watercolor painting depicting God, by <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a> (1757–1827)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">Deism</a> holds that God is wholly <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendent</a>: God exists, but does not intervene in the world beyond what was necessary to create it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lemos_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-lemos-42">[42]</a></sup> In this view, God is not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropomorphic" title="Anthropomorphic">anthropomorphic</a>, and neither answers prayers nor produces miracles. Common in Deism is a belief that God has no interest in humanity and may not even be aware of humanity. <a href="/wiki/Pandeism" title="Pandeism">Pandeism</a> combines Deism with Pantheistic beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawe_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dawe-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Pandeism is proposed to explain as to Deism why God would create a universe and then abandon it,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller-47">[47]</a></sup> and as to Pantheism, the origin and purpose of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">Pantheism</a> holds that God is the universe and the universe is God, whereas <a href="/wiki/Panentheism" title="Panentheism">Panentheism</a> holds that God contains, but is not identical to, the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> It is also the view of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Catholic_Church" title="Liberal Catholic Church">Liberal Catholic Church</a>; <a href="/wiki/Theosophy" title="Theosophy">Theosophy</a>; some views of Hinduism except <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavism</a>, which believes in panentheism; Sikhism; some divisions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neopaganism" title="Neopaganism">Neopaganism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, along with many varying denominations and individuals within denominations. <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a>, Jewish mysticism, paints a pantheistic/panentheistic view of God—which has wide acceptance in <a href="/wiki/Hasidic_Judaism" title="Hasidic Judaism">Hasidic Judaism</a>, particularly from their founder <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Israel_ben_Eliezer" title="Israel ben Eliezer">The Baal Shem Tov</a>—but only as an addition to the Jewish view of a personal god, not in the original pantheistic sense that denies or limits persona to God.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_concepts">Other concepts</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dystheism" title="Dystheism">Dystheism</a>, which is related to <a href="/wiki/Theodicy" title="Theodicy">theodicy</a>, is a form of theism which holds that God is either not wholly good or is fully malevolent as a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">problem of evil</a>. One such example comes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dostoevsky" title="Dostoevsky">Dostoevsky</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brothers_Karamazov" title="The Brothers Karamazov">The Brothers Karamazov</a></i>, in which Ivan Karamazov rejects God on the grounds that he allows children to suffer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In modern times, some more abstract concepts have been developed, such as <a href="/wiki/Process_theology" title="Process theology">process theology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Open_theism" title="Open theism">open theism</a>. The contemporaneous French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Michel_Henry" title="Michel Henry">Michel Henry</a> has however proposed a <a href="/wiki/Phenomenological_definition_of_God" title="Phenomenological definition of God">phenomenological approach and definition of God</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(religion)" title="Phenomenology (religion)">phenomenological</a> essence of <a href="/wiki/Phenomenological_life" title="Phenomenological life">Life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>God has also been conceived as being <a href="/wiki/Incorporeality" title="Incorporeality">incorporeal</a> (immaterial), a <a href="/wiki/Personal_god" title="Personal god">personal</a> being, the source of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moral_obligation" title="Moral obligation">moral obligation</a>, and the "greatest conceivable existent".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-3">[3]</a></sup> These attributes were all supported to varying degrees by the early <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jewish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Muslim</a> theologian philosophers, including <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-52">[52]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platinga_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Platinga-7">[7]</a></sup> respectively.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-theistic_views">Non-theistic views</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_origin_of_religions" title="Evolutionary origin of religions">Evolutionary origin of religions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_psychology_of_religion" title="Evolutionary psychology of religion">Evolutionary psychology of religion</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nontheism" title="Nontheism">Non-theist</a> views about God also vary. Some non-theists avoid the concept of God, whilst accepting that it is significant to many; other non-theists understand God as a symbol of human values and aspirations. The nineteenth-century English <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheist</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bradlaugh" title="Charles Bradlaugh">Charles Bradlaugh</a> declared that he refused to say "There is no God", because "the word 'God' is to me a sound conveying no clear or distinct affirmation";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> he said more specifically that he disbelieved in the Christian god. <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Jay_Gould" title="Stephen Jay Gould">Stephen Jay Gould</a> proposed an approach dividing the world of philosophy into what he called "<a href="/wiki/Non-overlapping_magisteria" title="Non-overlapping magisteria">non-overlapping magisteria</a>" (NOMA). In this view, questions of the <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>, such as those relating to the <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a> of God, are <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">non</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empirical" title="Empirical">empirical</a> and are the proper domain of <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>. The methods of science should then be used to answer any empirical question about the natural world, and theology should be used to answer questions about ultimate meaning and moral value. In this view, the perceived lack of any empirical footprint from the magisterium of the supernatural onto natural events makes science the sole player in the natural world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawkins-Delusion_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins-Delusion-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another view, advanced by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a>, is that the existence of God is an empirical question, on the grounds that "a universe with a god would be a completely different kind of universe from one without, and it would be a scientific difference."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawkins_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins-55">[55]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> argued that the doctrine of a Creator of the Universe was difficult to prove or disprove and that the only conceivable scientific discovery that could disprove the existence of a Creator (not necessarily a God) would be the discovery that the universe is infinitely old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a> and co-author <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Mlodinow" title="Leonard Mlodinow">Leonard Mlodinow</a> state in their book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Grand_Design_(book)" title="The Grand Design (book)">The Grand Design</a></i>, that it is reasonable to ask who or what created the universe, but if the answer is God, then the question has merely been deflected to that of who created God. Both authors claim however, that it is possible to answer these questions purely within the realm of science, and without invoking any divine beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agnosticism_and_atheism">Agnosticism and atheism</span></h3>
<p>Agnosticism is the view that, the <a href="/wiki/Truth_value" title="Truth value">truth values</a> of certain claims – especially <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a> and religious claims such as <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">whether God</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divine</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> exist – are unknown and perhaps unknowable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hepburn_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hepburn-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoweRoutledge_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-RoweRoutledge-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Atheism is, in a broad sense, the rejection of <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a> in the existence of <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deities</a>, or a God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In a narrower sense, atheism is specifically the position that there are no deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropomorphism">Anthropomorphism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">Anthropomorphism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pascal_Boyer" title="Pascal Boyer">Pascal Boyer</a> argues that while there is a wide array of supernatural concepts found around the world, in general, supernatural beings tend to behave much like people. The construction of gods and spirits like persons is one of the best known traits of religion. He cites examples from <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, which is, in his opinion, more like a modern <a href="/wiki/Soap_opera" title="Soap opera">soap opera</a> than other religious systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boyer_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-boyer-64">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_du_Castel" title="Bertrand du Castel">Bertrand du Castel</a> and Timothy Jurgensen demonstrate through formalization that Boyer's explanatory model matches physics' <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> in positing not directly observable entities as intermediaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ducasteljurgensen_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ducasteljurgensen-65">[65]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">Anthropologist</a> Stewart Guthrie contends that people project human features onto non-human aspects of the world because it makes those aspects more familiar. <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> also suggested that god concepts are projections of one's father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Likewise, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Durkheim" title="Émile Durkheim">Émile Durkheim</a> was one of the earliest to suggest that gods represent an extension of human social life to include supernatural beings. In line with this reasoning, psychologist Matt Rossano contends that when humans began living in larger groups, they may have created gods as a means of enforcing morality. In small groups, morality can be enforced by social forces such as gossip or reputation. However, it is much harder to enforce morality using social forces in much larger groups. Rossano indicates that by including ever-watchful gods and spirits, humans discovered an effective strategy for restraining selfishness and building more cooperative groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supernature_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-supernature-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Existence">Existence</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">Existence of God</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:St-thomas-aquinas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4226" data-file-width="2813" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/St-thomas-aquinas.jpg/200px-St-thomas-aquinas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/St-thomas-aquinas.jpg/300px-St-thomas-aquinas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/St-thomas-aquinas.jpg/400px-St-thomas-aquinas.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:St-thomas-aquinas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">St. Thomas Aquinas</a> summed up five main arguments as proofs for God's existence.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:121px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1074" data-file-width="780" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/119px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/179px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/238px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 2x" width="119"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> saw the existence of a Creator necessary in the movement of astronomical objects.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Arguments about the existence of God typically include empirical, deductive, and inductive types. Different views include that: "God does not exist" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_and_strong_atheism" title="Weak and strong atheism">strong atheism</a>); "God almost certainly does not exist" (<i>de facto</i> <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a>); "no one knows whether God exists" (<a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">agnosticism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>);"God exists, but this cannot be proven or disproven" (<i>de facto</i> <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theism</a>); and that "God exists and this can be proven" (<a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">strong theism</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawkins-Delusion_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins-Delusion-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Countless arguments have been proposed to prove the existence of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Some of the most notable arguments are the <a href="/wiki/Five_Ways_(Aquinas)" title="Five Ways (Aquinas)">Five Ways of Aquinas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Argument_from_desire" title="Argument from desire">Argument from desire</a> proposed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C.S._Lewis" title="C.S. Lewis">C.S. Lewis</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontological_Argument" title="Ontological Argument">Ontological Argument</a> formulated both by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Anselm" title="St. Anselm">St. Anselm</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes" title="Descartes">René Descartes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>St. Anselm's approach was to define God as, "that than which nothing greater can be conceived". Famed pantheist philosopher <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a> would later carry this idea to its extreme: "By God I understand a being absolutely infinite, i.e., a substance consisting of infinite attributes, of which each one expresses an eternal and infinite essence." For Spinoza, the whole of the natural universe is made of one substance, God, or its equivalent, Nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> His proof for the existence of God was a variation of the Ontological argument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">Scientist</a> <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> saw God as the masterful creator whose existence could not be denied in the face of the grandeur of all creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Nevertheless, he rejected polymath <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a>' thesis that God would necessarily make a perfect world which requires no intervention from the creator. In Query 31 of the <i>Opticks</i>, Newton simultaneously made an argument from design and for the necessity of intervention:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>For while comets move in very eccentric orbs in all manner of positions, blind fate could never make all the planets move one and the same way in orbs concentric, some inconsiderable irregularities excepted which may have arisen from the mutual actions of comets and planets on one another, and which will be apt to increase, till this system wants a reformation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>St. Thomas believed that the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a> is self-evident in itself, but not to us. "Therefore I say that this proposition, "God exists", of itself is self-evident, for the predicate is the same as the subject.... Now because we do not know the essence of God, the proposition is not self-evident to us; but needs to be demonstrated by things that are more known to us, though less known in their nature—namely, by effects."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> St. Thomas believed that the existence of God can be demonstrated. Briefly in the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa theologiae</a></i> and more extensively in the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_contra_Gentiles" title="Summa contra Gentiles">Summa contra Gentiles</a></i>, he considered in great detail five arguments for the existence of God, widely known as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quinque_viae" title="Quinque viae">quinque viae</a></i> (Five Ways).</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the original text of the five proofs, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quinque_viae" title="Quinque viae">quinque viae</a></div>
<ol>
<li>Motion: Some things undoubtedly move, though cannot cause their own motion. Since there can be no infinite chain of causes of motion, there must be a <a href="/wiki/Unmoved_mover" title="Unmoved mover">First Mover</a> not moved by anything else, and this is what everyone understands by God.</li>
<li>Causation: As in the case of motion, nothing can cause itself, and an infinite chain of causation is impossible, so there must be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prima_causa" title="Prima causa">First Cause</a>, called God.</li>
<li>Existence of necessary and the unnecessary: Our experience includes things certainly existing but apparently unnecessary. Not everything can be unnecessary, for then once there was nothing and there would still be nothing. Therefore, we are compelled to suppose something that exists necessarily, having this necessity only from itself; in fact itself the cause for other things to exist.</li>
<li>Gradation: If we can notice a gradation in things in the sense that some things are more hot, good, etc., there must be a superlative that is the truest and noblest thing, and so most fully existing. This then, we call God (Note: Thomas does not ascribe actual qualities to God Himself).</li>
<li>Ordered tendencies of nature: A direction of actions to an end is noticed in all bodies following natural laws. Anything without awareness tends to a goal under the guidance of one who is aware. This we call God (Note that even when we guide objects, in Thomas's view, the source of all our knowledge comes from God as well).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alister_McGrath.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="468" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Alister_McGrath.jpg/140px-Alister_McGrath.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Alister_McGrath.jpg/209px-Alister_McGrath.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Alister_McGrath.jpg/279px-Alister_McGrath.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alister_McGrath.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Alister_McGrath" title="Alister McGrath">Alister McGrath</a>, a formerly atheistic <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theologian</a> who has been highly critical of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a>' version of <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some theologians, such as the scientist and theologian <a href="/wiki/Alister_McGrath" title="Alister McGrath">A.E. McGrath</a>, argue that the existence of God is not a question that can be answered using the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgrath2005_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcgrath2005-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barackman2001_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-barackman2001-78">[78]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agnostic" title="Agnostic">Agnostic</a> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Jay_Gould" title="Stephen Jay Gould">Stephen Jay Gould</a> argues that science and religion are not in conflict and do not <a href="/wiki/Non-overlapping_magisteria" title="Non-overlapping magisteria">overlap</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some findings in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biology" title="Evolutionary biology">evolutionary biology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a> are interpreted by some atheists (including <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_M._Krauss" title="Lawrence M. Krauss">Lawrence M. Krauss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sam_Harris" title="Sam Harris">Sam Harris</a>) as evidence that God is an imaginary entity only, with no basis in reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris.2C_S_2005_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris.2C_S_2005-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> These atheists claim that a single, omniscient God who is imagined to have created the universe and is particularly attentive to the lives of humans has been imagined, embellished and promulgated in a trans-generational manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a> interprets such findings not only as a lack of evidence for the material existence of such a God, but as extensive evidence to the contrary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawkins-Delusion_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins-Delusion-54">[54]</a></sup> However, his views are opposed by some theologians and scientists including <a href="/wiki/Alister_McGrath" title="Alister McGrath">Alister McGrath</a>, who argues that existence of God is compatible with science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specific_attributes">Specific attributes</span></h2>
<p>Different religious traditions assign differing (though often similar) attributes and characteristics to God, including expansive powers and abilities, psychological characteristics, gender characteristics, and preferred nomenclature. The assignment of these attributes often differs according to the <a href="/wiki/Conceptions_of_God" title="Conceptions of God">conceptions of God</a> in the culture from which they arise. For example, <a href="/wiki/Attributes_of_God_in_Christianity" title="Attributes of God in Christianity">attributes of God in Christianity</a>, attributes of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God in Islam</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Attributes_of_Mercy" title="Thirteen Attributes of Mercy">the Thirteen Attributes of Mercy in Judaism</a> share certain similarities arising from their common roots.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God" title="Names of God">Names of God</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allah_Names_in_Chinese_Arabic_Script.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="200" height="400" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Allah_Names_in_Chinese_Arabic_Script.jpg/160px-Allah_Names_in_Chinese_Arabic_Script.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/Allah_Names_in_Chinese_Arabic_Script.jpg 1.5x" width="160"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allah_Names_in_Chinese_Arabic_Script.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
99 names of <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> <a href="/wiki/Sini_(script)" title="Sini (script)">Sini (script)</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The word <i>God</i> is "one of the most complex and difficult in the English language." In the <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Christian" title="Judeo-Christian">Judeo-Christian</a> tradition, "the Bible has been the principal source of the conceptions of God". That the Bible "includes many different images, concepts, and ways of thinking about" God has resulted in perpetual "disagreements about how God is to be conceived and understood".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout the Hebrew and Christian Bibles there are many names for God. One of them is Elohim. Another one is <i>El Shaddai</i>, translated "God Almighty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> A third notable name is <i>El Elyon</i>, which means "The High God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>God is described and referred in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> by certain names or attributes, the most common being <i><a href="/wiki/R-%E1%B8%A4-M" title="R-Ḥ-M">Al-Rahman</a></i>, meaning "Most Compassionate" and <i>Al-Rahim</i>, meaning "Most Merciful" (See <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Islam" title="Names of God in Islam">Names of God in Islam</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ben_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ben-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Das_Bild_der_H%C3%B6chsten_Seele_als_Lichtpunkt_TR.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="1296" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Das_Bild_der_H%C3%B6chsten_Seele_als_Lichtpunkt_TR.jpg/220px-Das_Bild_der_H%C3%B6chsten_Seele_als_Lichtpunkt_TR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Das_Bild_der_H%C3%B6chsten_Seele_als_Lichtpunkt_TR.jpg/330px-Das_Bild_der_H%C3%B6chsten_Seele_als_Lichtpunkt_TR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Das_Bild_der_H%C3%B6chsten_Seele_als_Lichtpunkt_TR.jpg/440px-Das_Bild_der_H%C3%B6chsten_Seele_als_Lichtpunkt_TR.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Das_Bild_der_H%C3%B6chsten_Seele_als_Lichtpunkt_TR.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Supreme soul</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Brahma_Kumaris" title="Brahma Kumaris">Brahma Kumaris</a> use the term "Supreme Soul" to refer to God. They see God as incorporeal and eternal, and regard him as a point of living light like human souls, but without a physical body, as he does not enter the cycle of birth, death and rebirth. God is seen as the perfect and constant embodiment of all virtues, powers and values and that He is the unconditionally loving Father of all souls, irrespective of their religion, gender, or culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramsay_Custodians_of_Purity4_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramsay_Custodians_of_Purity4-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavism</a>, a tradition in Hinduism, has <a href="/wiki/List_of_titles_and_names_of_Krishna" title="List of titles and names of Krishna">list of titles and names of Krishna</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender">Gender</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gender_of_God" title="Gender of God">Gender of God</a></div>
<p>The gender of God may be viewed as either a literal or an <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegorical</a> aspect of a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a> who, in classical western philosophy, transcends bodily form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytheistic" title="Polytheistic">Polytheistic</a> religions commonly attribute to each of <i>the gods</i> a gender, allowing each to interact with any of the others, and perhaps with humans, sexually. In most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotheistic" title="Monotheistic">monotheistic</a> religions, God has no counterpart with which to relate sexually. Thus, in classical western philosophy the <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a> of this one-and-only deity is most likely to be an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analogical" title="Analogical">analogical</a> statement of how humans and God address, and relate to, each other. Namely, God is seen as begetter of the world and revelation which corresponds to the active (as opposed to the receptive) role in sexual intercourse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Biblical sources usually refer to God using male words, except <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Genesis#1:26" title="s:Bible (King James)/Genesis">Genesis 1:26–27</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Psalms#123:2" title="s:Bible (King James)/Psalms">Psalm 123:2–3</a>, and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Luke#15:8" title="s:Bible (King James)/Luke">Luke 15:8–10</a> (female); <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Hosea#11:3" title="s:Bible (King James)/Hosea">Hosea 11:3–4</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Deuteronomy#32:18" title="s:Bible (King James)/Deuteronomy">Deuteronomy 32:18</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Isaiah#66:13" title="s:Bible (King James)/Isaiah">Isaiah 66:13</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Isaiah#49:15" title="s:Bible (King James)/Isaiah">Isaiah 49:15</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Isaiah#42:14" title="s:Bible (King James)/Isaiah">Isaiah 42:14</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Psalms#131:2" title="s:Bible (King James)/Psalms">Psalm 131:2</a> (a mother); <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Deuteronomy#32:11" title="s:Bible (King James)/Deuteronomy">Deuteronomy 32:11–12</a> (a mother eagle); and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Matthew#23:37" title="s:Bible (King James)/Matthew">Matthew 23:37</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Luke#13:34" title="s:Bible (King James)/Luke">Luke 13:34</a> (a mother hen).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_creation">Relationship with creation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">Creator deity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">Prayer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">Worship</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_Blake_008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1641" data-file-width="2024" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/William_Blake_008.jpg/220px-William_Blake_008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/William_Blake_008.jpg/330px-William_Blake_008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/William_Blake_008.jpg/440px-William_Blake_008.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William_Blake_008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>And Elohim Created Adam</i> by <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a>, c.1795</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">Prayer</a> plays a significant role among many believers. Muslims believe that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Purpose_of_life" title="Purpose of life">purpose of existence</a> is to <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">worship</a> God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-patheos1_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-patheos1-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> He is viewed as a personal God and there are no intermediaries, such as <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">clergy</a>, to contact God. Prayer often also includes <a href="/wiki/Supplication" title="Supplication">supplication</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forgiveness#Religious_views" title="Forgiveness">asking forgiveness</a>. God is often believed to be forgiving. For example, a <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> states God would replace a sinless people with one who sinned but still asked repentance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Christian theologian <a href="/wiki/Alister_McGrath" title="Alister McGrath">Alister McGrath</a> writes that there are good reasons to suggest that a "personal god" is integral to the Christian outlook, but that one has to understand it is an analogy. "To say that God is like a person is to affirm the divine ability and willingness to relate to others. This does not imply that God is human, or located at a specific point in the universe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adherents of different religions generally disagree as to how to best <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">worship</a> God and what is <a href="/wiki/Divine_providence" title="Divine providence">God's plan</a> for mankind, if there is one. There are different approaches to reconciling the contradictory claims of monotheistic religions. One view is taken by exclusivists, who believe they are the <a href="/wiki/Chosen_people" title="Chosen people">chosen people</a> or have exclusive access to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_truth" title="Absolute truth">absolute truth</a>, generally through <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a> or encounter with the Divine, which adherents of other religions do not. Another view is <a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">religious pluralism</a>. A pluralist typically believes that his religion is the right one, but does not deny the partial truth of other religions. An example of a pluralist view in Christianity is <a href="/wiki/Supersessionism" title="Supersessionism">supersessionism</a>, i.e., the belief that one's religion is the fulfillment of previous religions. A third approach is <a href="/wiki/Inclusivism" title="Inclusivism">relativistic inclusivism</a>, where everybody is seen as equally right; an example being <a href="/wiki/Universalism" title="Universalism">universalism</a>: the doctrine that <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> is eventually available for everyone. A fourth approach is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syncretic" title="Syncretic">syncretism</a>, mixing different elements from different religions. An example of syncretism is the <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> movement.</p>
<p>Jews and Christians believe that humans are created in the likeness of God, and are the center, crown and key to God's creation, <a href="/wiki/Stewardship_(theology)" title="Stewardship (theology)">stewards</a> for God, supreme over everything else God had made (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Gen+1%3A26&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Gen 1:26</a></span>); for this reason, humans are in Christianity called the "Children of God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Depiction">Depiction</span></h2>
<p>God is defined as incorporeal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-3">[3]</a></sup> and invisible from direct sight, and thus cannot be portrayed in a literal visual image.</p>
<p>The respective principles of religions may or may not permit them to use images (which are entirely symbolic) to represent God in art or in worship .</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1038" data-file-width="1574" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg/220px-Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg/330px-Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg/440px-Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Naqsh_i_Rustam._Investiture_d%27Ardashir_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ahura Mazda (depiction is on the right, with high crown) presents <a href="/wiki/Ardashir_I" title="Ardashir I">Ardashir I</a> (left) with the ring of kingship. (Relief at <a href="/wiki/Naqsh-e_Rustam" title="Naqsh-e Rustam">Naqsh-e Rustam</a>, 3rd century CE)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the early Parthian Empire, Ahura Mazda was visually represented for worship. This practice ended during the beginning of the Sassanid empire. Zoroastrian <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">iconoclasm</a>, which can be traced to the end of the Parthian period and the beginning of the Sassanid, eventually put an end to the use of all images of Ahura Mazda in worship. However, Ahura Mazda continued to be symbolized by a dignified male figure, standing or on horseback which is found in Sassanian investiture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyce1983686_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyce1983686-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God in Islam</a></div>
<p>Muslims believe that God (Allah) is beyond all comprehension or equal and does not resemble any of His creations in any way. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> are not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iconodules" title="Iconodules">iconodules</a>, are not expected to visualize God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">Judaism</span></h3>
<p>At least some Jews do not use any image for God, since God is the unimaginable Being who cannot be represented in material forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> In some samples of Jewish Art, however, sometimes God, or at least His Intervention, is indicated by a Hand Of God symbol, which represents the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bath_Kol" title="Bath Kol">bath Kol</a></i> (literally "daughter of a voice") or Voice of God;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-102">[102]</a></sup>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father_in_Western_art" title="God the Father in Western art">God the Father in Western art</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-overly_detailed" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>may contain an excessive amount of <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#Wikipedia_is_not_an_indiscriminate_collection_of_information" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">intricate detail</a> that may only interest a specific audience</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help by <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Content_forking#Article_spinoffs:_.22Summary_style.22_meta-articles_and_summary_sections" title="Wikipedia:Content forking">spinning off</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Handling_trivia#Recommendations_for_handling_trivia" title="Wikipedia:Handling trivia">relocating</a> any relevant information, and removing excessive detail that may be against <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">Wikipedia's inclusion policy</a>.</span> <small><i>(April 2016)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Early Christians believed that the words of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a> 1:18: "No man has seen God at any time" and numerous other statements were meant to apply not only to God, but to all attempts at the depiction of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James_Cornwell_page_24_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-James_Cornwell_page_24-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enluminure_Drogon_c.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="507" data-file-width="371" height="301" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Enluminure_Drogon_c.jpg/220px-Enluminure_Drogon_c.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Enluminure_Drogon_c.jpg/330px-Enluminure_Drogon_c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a6/Enluminure_Drogon_c.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enluminure_Drogon_c.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Use of the symbolic <a href="/wiki/Hand_of_God_(art)" title="Hand of God (art)">Hand of God</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Christ" title="Ascension of Christ">Ascension</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Drogo_Sacramentary" title="Drogo Sacramentary">Drogo Sacramentary</a>, c. 850</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>However, later depictions are found. Some, like the <a href="/wiki/Hand_of_God_(art)" title="Hand of God (art)">Hand of God</a>, are depiction borrowed from Jewish art.</p>
<p>The beginning of the 8th century witnessed the suppression and destruction of religious icons as the period of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_iconoclasm" title="Byzantine iconoclasm">Byzantine iconoclasm</a> (literally <i>image-breaking</i>) started. The <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">Second Council of Nicaea</a> in 787 effectively ended the first period of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_iconoclasm" title="Byzantine iconoclasm">Byzantine iconoclasm</a> and restored the honouring of icons and holy images in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> However, this did not immediately translate into large scale depictions of God the Father.
