Even supporters of the use of icons in the 8th century, such as Saint <a href="/wiki/John_of_Damascus" title="John of Damascus">John of Damascus</a>, drew a distinction between images of God the Father and those of Christ.</p>
<p>Prior to the 10th century no attempt was made to use a human to symbolize <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">God the Father</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_art" title="Western art">Western art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James_Cornwell_page_24_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-James_Cornwell_page_24-103">[103]</a></sup> Yet, Western art eventually required some way to illustrate the presence of the Father, so through successive representations a set of artistic styles for symbolizing the Father using a man gradually emerged around the 10th century AD. A rationale for the use of a human is the belief that God created the soul of Man in the image of His own (thus allowing Human to transcend the other animals).</p>
<p>It appears that when early artists designed to represent God the Father, fear and awe restrained them from a usage of the whole human figure. Typically only a small part would be used as the image, usually the hand, or sometimes the face, but rarely a whole human. In many images, the figure of the Son supplants the Father, so a smaller portion of the person of the Father is depicted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adolphe_Napol.C3.A9on_Didron_pages_169_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adolphe_Napol.C3.A9on_Didron_pages_169-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the 12th century depictions of God the Father had started to appear in French <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscripts</a>, which as a less public form could often be more adventurous in their iconography, and in <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">stained glass</a> church windows in England. Initially the head or bust was usually shown in some form of frame of clouds in the top of the picture space, where the Hand of God had formerly appeared; the <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Jesus" title="Baptism of Jesus">Baptism of Christ</a> on the famous <a href="/wiki/Baptismal_font_at_St_Bartholomew%27s_Church,_Li%C3%A8ge" title="Baptismal font at St Bartholomew's Church, Liège">baptismal font in Liège</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainer_of_Huy" title="Rainer of Huy">Rainer of Huy</a> is an example from 1118 (a Hand of God is used in another scene). Gradually the amount of the human symbol shown can increase to a half-length figure, then a full-length, usually enthroned, as in <a href="/wiki/Giotto" title="Giotto">Giotto</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">fresco</a> of c. 1305 in <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-106">[106]</a></sup> In the 14th century the <a href="/wiki/Naples_Bible" title="Naples Bible">Naples Bible</a> carried a depiction of God the Father in the <a href="/wiki/Burning_bush" title="Burning bush">Burning bush</a>. By the early 15th century, the <a href="/wiki/Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_Duc_de_Berry" title="Très Riches Heures du Duc de Berry">Très Riches Heures du Duc de Berry</a> has a considerable number of symbols, including an elderly but tall and elegant full-length figure walking in the <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden" title="Garden of Eden">Garden of Eden</a>, which show a considerable diversity of apparent ages and dress. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battistero_di_San_Giovanni_(Florence)#Lorenzo_Ghiberti" title="Battistero di San Giovanni (Florence)">"Gates of Paradise" of the Florence Baptistry</a> by <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Ghiberti" title="Lorenzo Ghiberti">Lorenzo Ghiberti</a>, begun in 1425 use a similar tall full-length symbol for the Father. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rohan_Book_of_Hours" title="Rohan Book of Hours">Rohan Book of Hours</a> of about 1430 also included depictions of God the Father in half-length human form, which were now becoming standard, and the Hand of God becoming rarer. At the same period other works, like the large Genesis <a href="/wiki/Altarpiece" title="Altarpiece">altarpiece</a> by the Hamburg painter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meister_Bertram" title="Meister Bertram">Meister Bertram</a>, continued to use the old depiction of Christ as <i>Logos</i> in Genesis scenes. In the 15th century there was a brief fashion for depicting all three persons of the Trinity as <a href="/wiki/Trinity#Less_common_types_of_depiction" title="Trinity">similar or identical figures with the usual appearance of Christ</a>.</p>
<p>In an early Venetian school <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_the_Virgin" title="Coronation of the Virgin">Coronation of the Virgin</a> by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_d%27Alemagna" title="Giovanni d'Alemagna">Giovanni d'Alemagna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Vivarini" title="Antonio Vivarini">Antonio Vivarini</a>, (c. 1443) The Father is depicted using the symbol consistently used by other artists later, namely a patriarch, with benign, yet powerful countenance and with long white hair and a beard, a depiction largely derived from, and justified by, the near-physical, but still figurative, description of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_of_Days" title="Ancient of Days">Ancient of Days</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>. ...the Ancient of Days did sit, whose garment was white as snow, and the hair of his head like the pure wool: his throne was like the fiery flame, and his wheels as burning fire. (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Daniel" title="Book of Daniel">Daniel</a> 7:9)</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andrea_del_Verrocchio,_Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_Baptism_of_Christ_-_Uffizi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1864" data-file-width="1565" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Andrea_del_Verrocchio%2C_Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_Baptism_of_Christ_-_Uffizi.jpg/220px-Andrea_del_Verrocchio%2C_Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_Baptism_of_Christ_-_Uffizi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Andrea_del_Verrocchio%2C_Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_Baptism_of_Christ_-_Uffizi.jpg/330px-Andrea_del_Verrocchio%2C_Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_Baptism_of_Christ_-_Uffizi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Andrea_del_Verrocchio%2C_Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_Baptism_of_Christ_-_Uffizi.jpg/440px-Andrea_del_Verrocchio%2C_Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_Baptism_of_Christ_-_Uffizi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Andrea_del_Verrocchio,_Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_Baptism_of_Christ_-_Uffizi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Usage of two Hands of God (relatively unusual) and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> as a dove in <a href="/wiki/The_Baptism_of_Christ_(Verrocchio)" title="The Baptism of Christ (Verrocchio)">Baptism of Christ, by Verrocchio</a>, 1472</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the Annunciation by <a href="/wiki/Benvenuto_di_Giovanni" title="Benvenuto di Giovanni">Benvenuto di Giovanni</a> in 1470, God the Father is portrayed in the red robe and a hat that resembles that of a Cardinal. However, even in the later part of the 15th century, the symbolic representation of the Father and the Holy Spirit as "hands and dove" continued, e.g. in <a href="/wiki/The_Baptism_of_Christ_(Verrocchio)" title="The Baptism of Christ (Verrocchio)">Verrocchio's Baptism of Christ</a> in 1472.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:God_the_Father_with_His_Right_Hand_Raised_in_Blessing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="390" data-file-width="450" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/God_the_Father_with_His_Right_Hand_Raised_in_Blessing.jpg/220px-God_the_Father_with_His_Right_Hand_Raised_in_Blessing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/God_the_Father_with_His_Right_Hand_Raised_in_Blessing.jpg/330px-God_the_Father_with_His_Right_Hand_Raised_in_Blessing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/God_the_Father_with_His_Right_Hand_Raised_in_Blessing.jpg/440px-God_the_Father_with_His_Right_Hand_Raised_in_Blessing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:God_the_Father_with_His_Right_Hand_Raised_in_Blessing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>God the Father with His Right Hand Raised in Blessing</i>, with a triangular halo representing the Trinity, <a href="/wiki/Girolamo_dai_Libri" title="Girolamo dai Libri">Girolamo dai Libri</a> c. 1555</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In Renaissance paintings of the adoration of the Trinity, God may be depicted in two ways, either with emphasis on The Father, or the three elements of the Trinity. The most usual depiction of the Trinity in Renaissance art depicts God the Father using an old man, usually with a long beard and patriarchal in appearance, sometimes with a triangular halo (as a reference to the Trinity), or with a papal crown, specially in Northern Renaissance painting. In these depictions The Father may hold a globe or book (to symbolize God's knowledge and as a reference to how knowledge is deemed divine). He is behind and above Christ on the Cross in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Throne_of_Mercy" title="Throne of Mercy">Throne of Mercy</a> iconography. A dove, the symbol of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> may hover above. Various people from different classes of society, e.g. kings, popes or martyrs may be present in the picture. In a Trinitarian <a href="/wiki/Piet%C3%A0" title="Pietà">Pietà</a>, God the Father is often symbolized using a man wearing a papal dress and a papal crown, supporting the dead Christ in his arms. They are depicted as floating in heaven with angels who carry the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Instruments_of_the_Passion" title="Instruments of the Passion">instruments of the Passion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Representations of God the Father and the Trinity were attacked both by Protestants and within Catholicism, by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jansenist" title="Jansenist">Jansenist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baianist" title="Baianist">Baianist</a> movements as well as more orthodox theologians. As with other attacks on Catholic imagery, this had the effect both of reducing Church support for the less central depictions, and strengthening it for the core ones. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Church" title="Western Church">Western Church</a>, the pressure to restrain religious imagery resulted in the highly influential decrees of the final session of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a> in 1563. The Council of Trent decrees confirmed the traditional Catholic doctrine that images only represented the person depicted, and that veneration to them was paid to the person, not the image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Artistic depictions of God the Father were uncontroversial in Catholic art thereafter, but less common depictions of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> were condemned. In 1745 <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XIV" title="Pope Benedict XIV">Pope Benedict XIV</a> explicitly supported the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Throne_of_Mercy" title="Throne of Mercy">Throne of Mercy</a> depiction, referring to the "Ancient of Days", but in 1786 it was still necessary for <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_VI" title="Pope Pius VI">Pope Pius VI</a> to issue a <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">papal bull</a> condemning the decision of an Italian church council to remove all images of the Trinity from churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Creation_of_Adam.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="741" data-file-width="1614" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/The_Creation_of_Adam.jpg/500px-The_Creation_of_Adam.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/The_Creation_of_Adam.jpg/750px-The_Creation_of_Adam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/The_Creation_of_Adam.jpg/1000px-The_Creation_of_Adam.jpg 2x" width="500"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Creation_of_Adam.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The famous <i><a href="/wiki/The_Creation_of_Adam" title="The Creation of Adam">The Creation of Adam</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>, c.1512</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>God the Father is symbolized in several Genesis scenes in <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel_ceiling" title="Sistine Chapel ceiling">Sistine Chapel ceiling</a>, most famously <i><a href="/wiki/The_Creation_of_Adam" title="The Creation of Adam">The Creation of Adam</a></i> (whose image of near touching hands of God and Adam is iconic of humanity, being a reminder that Man is created in the Image and Likeness of God (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Gen+1%3A26&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Gen 1:26</a></span>)).God the Father is depicted as a powerful figure, floating in the clouds in <a href="/wiki/Assumption_of_the_Virgin_(Titian)" title="Assumption of the Virgin (Titian)">Titian's Assumption of the Virgin</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frari_of_Venice" title="Frari of Venice">Frari of Venice</a>, long admired as a masterpiece of <a href="/wiki/High_Renaissance" title="High Renaissance">High Renaissance</a> art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Ges%C3%B9" title="Church of the Gesù">Church of the Gesù</a> in Rome includes a number of 16th century depictions of <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">God the Father</a>. In some of these paintings the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> is still alluded to in terms of three angels, but <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Fiammeri" title="Giovanni Battista Fiammeri">Giovanni Battista Fiammeri</a> also depicted God the Father as a man riding on a cloud, above the scenes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>In both the Last Judgment and the Coronation of the Virgin paintings by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rubens" title="Rubens">Rubens</a> he depicted God the Father using the image that by then had become widely accepted, a bearded patriarchal figure above the fray. In the 17th century, the two Spanish artists <a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Velázquez">Velázquez</a> (whose father-in-law <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Pacheco" title="Francisco Pacheco">Francisco Pacheco</a> was in charge of the approval of new images for the Inquisition) and <a href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_Esteban_Murillo" title="Bartolomé Esteban Murillo">Murillo</a> both depicted God the Father using a patriarchal figure with a white beard in a purple robe.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_a_Prophecy,_copy_D,_object_1_(Bentley_1,_Erdman_i,_Keynes_i)_British_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2800" data-file-width="2057" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Europe_a_Prophecy%2C_copy_D%2C_object_1_%28Bentley_1%2C_Erdman_i%2C_Keynes_i%29_British_Museum.jpg/220px-Europe_a_Prophecy%2C_copy_D%2C_object_1_%28Bentley_1%2C_Erdman_i%2C_Keynes_i%29_British_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Europe_a_Prophecy%2C_copy_D%2C_object_1_%28Bentley_1%2C_Erdman_i%2C_Keynes_i%29_British_Museum.jpg/330px-Europe_a_Prophecy%2C_copy_D%2C_object_1_%28Bentley_1%2C_Erdman_i%2C_Keynes_i%29_British_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Europe_a_Prophecy%2C_copy_D%2C_object_1_%28Bentley_1%2C_Erdman_i%2C_Keynes_i%29_British_Museum.jpg/440px-Europe_a_Prophecy%2C_copy_D%2C_object_1_%28Bentley_1%2C_Erdman_i%2C_Keynes_i%29_British_Museum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_a_Prophecy,_copy_D,_object_1_(Bentley_1,_Erdman_i,_Keynes_i)_British_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Ancient of Days</i> (1794) Watercolor etching by <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While representations of God the Father were growing in Italy, Spain, Germany and the Low Countries, there was resistance elsewhere in Europe, even during the 17th century. In 1632 most members of the <a href="/wiki/Star_Chamber" title="Star Chamber">Star Chamber</a> court in England (except the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">Archbishop of York</a>) condemned the use of the images of the Trinity in church windows, and some considered them illegal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Later in the 17th century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Thomas_Browne" title="Sir Thomas Browne">Sir Thomas Browne</a> wrote that he considered the representation of God the Father using an old man "a dangerous act" that might lead to Egyptian symbolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-115">[115]</a></sup> In 1847, Charles Winston was still critical of such images as a "<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romish" title="Romish">Romish trend</a></i>" (a term used to refer to <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_(term)" title="Roman Catholic (term)">Roman Catholics</a>) that he considered best avoided in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1667 the 43rd chapter of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moscow_Sobor_of_1666%E2%80%931667" title="Moscow Sobor of 1666–1667">Great Moscow Council</a> specifically included a ban on a number of symbolic depictions of God the Father and the Holy Spirit, which then also resulted in a whole range of other icons being placed on the forbidden list,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> mostly affecting Western-style depictions which had been gaining ground in Orthodox icons. The Council also declared that the person of the Trinity who was the "Ancient of Days" was Christ, as <i>Logos</i>, not God the Father. However some icons continued to be produced in Russia, as well as <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, and other Orthodox countries.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theological_approaches">Theological approaches</span></h2>
<p>Theologians and philosophers have attributed to God such characteristics as <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">omniscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">omnipotence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omnipresence" title="Omnipresence">omnipresence</a>, perfect <a href="/wiki/Good_and_evil" title="Good and evil">goodness</a>, divine <a href="/wiki/Simplicity" title="Simplicity">simplicity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eternity" title="Eternity">eternal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Necessary_Existent" title="Necessary Existent">necessary</a> existence. God has been described as <a href="/wiki/Incorporeality" title="Incorporeality">incorporeal</a>, a personal being, the source of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moral_obligation" title="Moral obligation">moral obligation</a>, and the greatest conceivable being existent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-3">[3]</a></sup> These attributes were all claimed to varying degrees by the early <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jewish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Muslim</a> scholars, including <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">St Augustine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-52">[52]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosophers</a> developed arguments for the existence of God,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platinga_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Platinga-7">[7]</a></sup> while attempting to comprehend the precise implications of God's attributes. Reconciling some of those attributes generated important philosophical problems and debates. For example, God's omniscience may seem to imply that God knows how free agents will choose to act. If God does know this, their ostensible <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a> might be illusory, or foreknowledge does not imply predestination, and if God does not know it, God may not be omniscient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wierenga_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wierenga-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>The last centuries of philosophy have seen vigorous questions regarding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arguments_for_the_existence_of_God" title="Arguments for the existence of God">arguments for God's existence</a> raised by such philosophers as <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antony_Flew" title="Antony Flew">Antony Flew</a>, although Kant held that the <a href="/wiki/Argument_from_morality" title="Argument from morality">argument from morality</a> was valid. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theist" title="Theist">theist</a> response has been either to contend, as does <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Plantinga" title="Alvin Plantinga">Alvin Plantinga</a>, that faith is "<a href="/wiki/Reformed_epistemology" title="Reformed epistemology">properly basic</a>", or to take, as does <a href="/wiki/Richard_Swinburne" title="Richard Swinburne">Richard Swinburne</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evidentialist" title="Evidentialist">evidentialist</a> position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theists</a> agree that only some of the arguments for God's existence are compelling, but argue that <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a> is not a product of <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>, but requires risk. There would be no risk, they say, if the arguments for God's existence were as solid as the laws of logic, a position summed up by <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">Pascal</a> as "the heart has reasons of which reason does not know."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many religious believers allow for the existence of other, less powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiritual_being" title="Spiritual being">spiritual beings</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">angels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jinn" title="Jinn">jinn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demon" title="Demon">demons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Deva_(New_Age)" title="Deva (New Age)">devas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"G-d has no body, no genitalia, therefore the very idea that G-d is male or female is patently absurd. Although in the Talmudic part of the Torah and especially in Kabalah G-d is referred to under the name '<a href="/wiki/Shekhinah" title="Shekhinah">Sh'chinah</a>' – which is feminine, this is only to accentuate the fact that all the creation and nature are actually in the receiving end in reference to the creator and as no part of the creation can perceive the creator outside of nature, it is adequate to refer to the divine presence in feminine form. We refer to G-d using masculine terms simply for convenience's sake, because Hebrew has no neutral gender; G-d is no more male than a table is." <a class="external text" href="http://www.jewfaq.org/g-d.htm" rel="nofollow">Judaism 101</a>. <li id="cite_note-Platinga-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Platinga_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Platinga_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Platinga_7-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Alvin_Plantinga" title="Alvin Plantinga">Platinga, Alvin</a>. <li id="cite_note-Lataster-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Lataster_8-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Raphael_Lataster" title="Raphael Lataster">Raphael Lataster</a> (2013). <i>There was no Jesus, there is no God: A Scholarly Examination of the Scientific, Historical, and Philosophical Evidence &amp; Arguments for Monotheism</i>. p. 165. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1492234419" title="Special:BookSources/1492234419">1492234419</a>. <q>This one god could be of the deistic or pantheistic sort. Deism might be superior in explaining why God has seemingly left us to our own devices and pantheism could be the more logical option as it fits well with the ontological argument's 'maximally-great entity' and doesn't rely on unproven concepts about 'nothing' (as in 'creation out of nothing'). A mixture of the two, pandeism, could be the most likely God-concept of all.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.au=Raphael+Lataster&amp;rft.btitle=There+was+no+Jesus%2C+there+is+no+God%3A+A+Scholarly+Examination+of+the+Scientific%2C+Historical%2C+and+Philosophical+Evidence+%26+Arguments+for+Monotheism&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1492234419&amp;rft.pages=165&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Dawe-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Dawe_9-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Dawe_9-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Alan H. Dawe (2011). <i>The God Franchise: A Theory of Everything</i>. p. 48. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0473201143" title="Special:BookSources/0473201143">0473201143</a>. <q>Pandeism: This is the belief that God created the universe, is now one with it, and so, is no longer a separate conscious entity. This is a combination of pantheism (God is identical to the universe) and deism (God created the universe and then withdrew Himself).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.au=Alan+H.+Dawe&amp;rft.btitle=The+God+Franchise%3A+A+Theory+of+Everything&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0473201143&amp;rft.pages=48&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The ulterior etymology is disputed. Apart from the unlikely hypothesis of adoption from a foreign tongue, the OTeut. "ghuba" implies as its preTeut-type either "*ghodho-m" or "*ghodto-m". The former does not appear to admit of explanation; but the latter would represent the neut. pple. of a root "gheu-". There are two Aryan roots of the required form ("*g,heu-" with palatal aspirate) one with meaning 'to invoke' (Skr. "hu") the other 'to pour, to offer sacrifice' (Skr "hu", Gr. χεηi;ν, OE "geotàn" Yete v). <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">OED Compact Edition, G, p. 267</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-BARNHART323-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-BARNHART323_24-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Barnhart, Robert K (1995). <i>The Barnhart Concise Dictionary of Etymology: the Origins of American English Words</i>, page 323. <a href="/wiki/HarperCollins" title="HarperCollins">HarperCollins</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-06-270084-7" title="Special:BookSources/0-06-270084-7">0-06-270084-7</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Webster%27s_New_World_Dictionary" title="Webster's New World Dictionary">Webster's New World Dictionary</a>; "God n. ME &lt; OE, akin to Ger gott, Goth guth, prob. &lt; IE base * ĝhau-, to call out to, invoke &gt; Sans havaté, (he) calls upon; 1. any of various beings conceived of as supernatural, immortal, and having special powers over the lives and affairs of people and the course of nature; deity, esp. a male deity: typically considered objects of worship; 2. an image that is worshiped; idol 3. a person or thing deified or excessively honored and admired; 4. [G-] in monotheistic religions, the creator and ruler of the universe, regarded as eternal, infinite, all-powerful, and all-knowing; <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Being" title="Supreme Being">Supreme Being</a>; the Almighty"</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://dictionary.reference.com/browse/God" rel="nofollow">Dictionary.com</a>; "God /gɒd/ noun: 1. the one Supreme Being, the creator and ruler of the universe. 2. the Supreme Being considered with reference to a particular attribute. 3. (lowercase) one of several deities, esp. a male deity, presiding over some portion of worldly affairs. 4. (often lowercase) a supreme being according to some particular conception: the God of mercy. 5. Christian Science. the Supreme Being, understood as Life, Truth, Love, Mind, Soul, Spirit, Principle. 6. (lowercase) an image of a deity; an idol. 7. (lowercase) any deified person or object. 8. (often lowercase) Gods, Theater. 8a. the upper balcony in a theater. 8b. the spectators in this part of the balcony."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-TFDtheism-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-TFDtheism_44-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.thefreedictionary.com/theism" rel="nofollow">"Theism – definition of theism by the Free Online Dictionary, Thesaurus and Encyclopedia"</a>. <a href="/wiki/TheFreeDictionary.com" title="TheFreeDictionary.com">TheFreeDictionary.com</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2008-07-16</span></span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.btitle=Theism+%E2%80%93+definition+of+theism+by+the+Free+Online+Dictionary%2C+Thesaurus+and+Encyclopedia&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pub=TheFreeDictionary.com&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.thefreedictionary.com%2Ftheism&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Sean F. Johnston (2009). <i>The History of Science: A Beginner's Guide</i>. p. 90. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-85168-681-9" title="Special:BookSources/1-85168-681-9">1-85168-681-9</a>. <q>In its most abstract form, deism may not attempt to describe the characteristics of such a non-interventionist creator, or even that the universe is identical with God (a variant known as pandeism).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.au=Sean+F.+Johnston&amp;rft.btitle=The+History+of+Science%3A+A+Beginner%27s+Guide&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1-85168-681-9&amp;rft.pages=90&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Paul Bradley (2011). <i>This Strange Eventful History: A Philosophy of Meaning</i>. p. 156. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0875868762" title="Special:BookSources/0875868762">0875868762</a>. <q>Pandeism combines the concepts of Deism and Pantheism with a god who creates the universe and then becomes it.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.au=Paul+Bradley&amp;rft.btitle=This+Strange+Eventful+History%3A+A+Philosophy+of+Meaning&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0875868762&amp;rft.pages=156&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Fuller-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Fuller_47-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Fuller_47-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Allan R. Fuller (2010). <i>Thought: The Only Reality</i>. p. 79. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1608445909" title="Special:BookSources/1608445909">1608445909</a>. <q>Pandeism is another belief that states that God is identical to the universe, but God no longer exists in a way where He can be contacted; therefore, this theory can only be proven to exist by reason. Pandeism views the entire universe as being from God and now the universe is the entirety of God, but the universe at some point in time will fold back into one single being which is God Himself that created all. Pandeism raises the question as to why would God create a universe and then abandon it? As this relates to pantheism, it raises the question of how did the universe come about what is its aim and purpose?</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.au=Allan+R.+Fuller&amp;rft.btitle=Thought%3A+The+Only+Reality&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1608445909&amp;rft.pages=79&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Peter C. Rogers (2009). <i>Ultimate Truth, Book 1</i>. p. 121. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1438979681" title="Special:BookSources/1438979681">1438979681</a>. <q>As with <a href="/wiki/Panentheism" title="Panentheism">Panentheism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">Pantheism</a> is derived from the Greek: 'pan'= all and 'theos' = God, it literally means "God is All" and "All is God." Pantheist purports that everything is part of an all-inclusive, indwelling, intangible God; or that the Universe, or nature, and God are the same. Further review helps to accentuate the idea that natural law, existence, and the Universe which is the sum total of all that is, was, and shall be, is represented in the theological principle of an abstract 'god' rather than an individual, creative Divine Being or Beings of any kind. This is the key element which distinguishes them from Panentheists and Pandeists. As such, although many religions may claim to hold <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheistic" title="Pantheistic">Pantheistic</a> elements, they are more commonly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panentheistic" title="Panentheistic">Panentheistic</a> or Pandeistic in nature.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.au=Peter+C.+Rogers&amp;rft.btitle=Ultimate+Truth%2C+Book+1&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1438979681&amp;rft.pages=121&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">John Culp (2013). <a class="external text" href="http://plato.stanford.edu/cgi-bin/encyclopedia/archinfo.cgi?entry=panentheism" rel="nofollow">"Panentheism,"</a> <i>Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</i>, Spring.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Sagan, Carl</a> (1996). <i>The Demon Haunted World p.278</i>. New York: Ballantine Books. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-345-40946-9" title="Special:BookSources/0-345-40946-9">0-345-40946-9</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.aufirst=Carl&amp;rft.aulast=Sagan&amp;rft.btitle=The+Demon+Haunted+World+p.278&amp;rft.date=1996&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-345-40946-9&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Ballantine+Books&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Stephen Hawking; Leonard Mlodinow (2010). <i>The Grand Design</i>. Bantam Books. p. 172. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-553-80537-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-553-80537-6">978-0-553-80537-6</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.au=Leonard+Mlodinow&amp;rft.au=Stephen+Hawking&amp;rft.btitle=The+Grand+Design&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-553-80537-6&amp;rft.pages=172&amp;rft.pub=Bantam+Books&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Hepburn-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Hepburn_58-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation encyclopaedia">Hepburn, Ronald W. (2005) [1967]. "Agnosticism". In Donald M. Borchert. <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Encyclopedia of Philosophy">The Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a></i>. <b>1</b> (2nd ed.). MacMillan Reference USA (Gale). p. 92. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-02-865780-2" title="Special:BookSources/0-02-865780-2">0-02-865780-2</a>. <q>In the most general use of the term, agnosticism is the view that we do not know whether there is a God or not.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.atitle=Agnosticism&amp;rft.aufirst=Ronald+W.&amp;rft.aulast=Hepburn&amp;rft.btitle=The+Encyclopedia+of+Philosophy&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.isbn=0-02-865780-2&amp;rft.pages=92&amp;rft.pub=MacMillan+Reference+USA+%28Gale%29&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> (page 56 in 1967 edition)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-RoweRoutledge-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-RoweRoutledge_59-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation encyclopaedia"><a href="/wiki/William_L._Rowe" title="William L. Rowe">Rowe, William L.</a> (1998). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/?id=VQ-GhVWTH84C&amp;pg=PA122&amp;dq=agnosticism+routledge" rel="nofollow">"Agnosticism"</a>. In Edward Craig. <i><a href="/wiki/Routledge_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Routledge Encyclopedia of Philosophy">Routledge Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a></i>. Taylor &amp; Francis. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-415-07310-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-415-07310-3">978-0-415-07310-3</a>. <q>In the popular sense, an agnostic is someone who neither believes nor disbelieves in God, whereas an atheist disbelieves in God. In the strict sense, however, agnosticism is the view that human reason is incapable of providing sufficient rational grounds to justify either the belief that God exists or the belief that God does not exist. In so far as one holds that our beliefs are rational only if they are sufficiently supported by human reason, the person who accepts the philosophical position of agnosticism will hold that neither the belief that God exists nor the belief that God does not exist is rational.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.atitle=Agnosticism&amp;rft.aufirst=William+L.&amp;rft.aulast=Rowe&amp;rft.btitle=Routledge+Encyclopedia+of+Philosophy&amp;rft.date=1998&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-415-07310-3&amp;rft.pub=Taylor+%26+Francis&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3DVQ-GhVWTH84C%26pg%3DPA122%26dq%3Dagnosticism%2Broutledge&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation encyclopaedia">"agnostic, agnosticism". <i>OED Online, 3rd ed</i>. Oxford University Press. September 2012. <q><b>agnostic</b>. : <b>A</b>. n[oun]. :# A person who believes that nothing is known or can be known of immaterial things, especially of the existence or nature of God. :# In extended use: a person who is not persuaded by or committed to a particular point of view; a sceptic. Also: person of indeterminate ideology or conviction; an equivocator. : <b>B.</b> adj[ective]. :# Of or relating to the belief that the existence of anything beyond and behind material phenomena is unknown and (as far as can be judged) unknowable. Also: holding this belief. :# a. In extended use: not committed to or persuaded by a particular point of view; sceptical. Also: politically or ideologically unaligned; non-partisan, equivocal. <b>agnosticism</b> n. The doctrine or tenets of agnostics with regard to the existence of anything beyond and behind material phenomena or to knowledge of a First Cause or God.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.atitle=agnostic%2C+agnosticism&amp;rft.btitle=OED+Online%2C+3rd+ed.&amp;rft.date=2012-09&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Nielsen 2013: "Instead of saying that an atheist is someone who believes that it is false or probably false that there is a God, a more adequate characterization of atheism consists in the more complex claim that to be an atheist is to be someone who rejects belief in God for the following reasons ... : for an anthropomorphic God, the atheist rejects belief in God because it is false or probably false that there is a God; for a nonanthropomorphic God ... because the concept of such a God is either meaningless, unintelligible, contradictory, incomprehensible, or incoherent; for the God portrayed by some modern or contemporary theologians or philosophers ... because the concept of God in question is such that it merely masks an atheistic substance—e.g., "God" is just another name for love, or ... a symbolic term for moral ideals."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Edwards 2005: "On our definition, an 'atheist' is a person who rejects belief in God, regardless of whether or not his reason for the rejection is the claim that 'God exists' expresses a false proposition. People frequently adopt an attitude of rejection toward a position for reasons other than that it is a false proposition. It is common among contemporary philosophers, and indeed it was not uncommon in earlier centuries, to reject positions on the ground that they are meaningless. Sometimes, too, a theory is rejected on such grounds as that it is sterile or redundant or capricious, and there are many other considerations which in certain contexts are generally agreed to constitute good grounds for rejecting an assertion."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Rowe 1998: "As commonly understood, atheism is the position that affirms the nonexistence of God. So an atheist is someone who disbelieves in God, whereas a theist is someone who believes in God. Another meaning of 'atheism' is simply nonbelief in the existence of God, rather than positive belief in the nonexistence of God. ... an atheist, in the broader sense of the term, is someone who disbelieves in every form of deity, not just the God of traditional Western theology."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-boyer-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-boyer_64-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Pascal_Boyer" title="Pascal Boyer">Boyer, Pascal</a> (2001). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/?id=wreF80OHTicC&amp;pg=PA142&amp;lpg=PA142&amp;dq=boyer+modern+soap+opera" rel="nofollow"><i>Religion Explained,</i></a>. New York: Basic Books. pp. 142–243. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-465-00696-5" title="Special:BookSources/0-465-00696-5">0-465-00696-5</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.aufirst=Pascal&amp;rft.aulast=Boyer&amp;rft.btitle=Religion+Explained%2C&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-465-00696-5&amp;rft.pages=142-243&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Basic+Books&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3DwreF80OHTicC%26pg%3DPA142%26lpg%3DPA142%26dq%3Dboyer%2Bmodern%2Bsoap%2Bopera&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-ducasteljurgensen-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ducasteljurgensen_65-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Bertrand_du_Castel" title="Bertrand du Castel">du Castel, Bertrand</a>; Jurgensen, Timothy M. <li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">Thomas Henry Huxley</a>, an English biologist, was the first to come up with the word <i>agnostic</i> in 1869 <cite class="citation book">Dixon, Thomas (2008). <i>Science and Religion: A Very Short Introduction</i>. Oxford: Oxford University Press. p. 63. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-929551-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-929551-7">978-0-19-929551-7</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.aufirst=Thomas&amp;rft.aulast=Dixon&amp;rft.btitle=Science+and+Religion%3A+A+Very+Short+Introduction&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-929551-7&amp;rft.pages=63&amp;rft.place=Oxford&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> However, earlier authors and published works have promoted an agnostic points of view. They include <a href="/wiki/Protagoras" title="Protagoras">Protagoras</a>, a 5th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Before_Common_Era" title="Before Common Era">BCE</a> Greek philosopher. <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.iep.utm.edu/p/protagor.htm" rel="nofollow">"The Internet Encyclopedia of Philosophy – Protagoras (c. 490 – c. 420 BCE)"</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20081014181706/http://www.iep.utm.edu/p/protagor.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 2008-10-14<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2008-10-06</span></span>. <q>While the pious might wish to look to the gods to provide absolute moral guidance in the relativistic universe of the Sophistic Enlightenment, that certainty also was cast into doubt by philosophic and sophistic thinkers, who pointed out the absurdity and immorality of the conventional epic accounts of the gods. Protagoras' prose treatise about the gods began 'Concerning the gods, I have no means of knowing whether they exist or not or of what sort they may be. Many things prevent knowledge including the obscurity of the subject and the brevity of human life.'</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.btitle=The+Internet+Encyclopedia+of+Philosophy+%E2%80%93+Protagoras+%28c.+490+%E2%80%93+c.+420+BCE%29&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.iep.utm.edu%2Fp%2Fprotagor.htm&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
